## Transcript

- [00:00] Heres a riddle, my Hobbitses: Whats something we all experience, all the time,
- [00:04] that we cant really measure, and barely have words to define?
- [00:07] You cant hold it in your hand, or take a bite out of it.
- [00:09] It isnt something you learn or practice; it just IS.
- [00:12] Consciousness.
- [00:13] Every science has certain concepts that are so fundamental, yet abstract, that we have
- [00:17] a hard time finding the appropriate words to describe them.
- [00:20] Ask a physicist and theyll tell you energy and space defy simple definitions.
- [00:25] Biologists know if something is alive, but have a harder time explaining what life actually
- [00:30] is.
- [00:31] Ask a psychologist what consciousness is, and youll getyoull get a slippery
- [00:35] answer.
- [00:36] For the purposes of this conversation, were going to actually loosely define consciousness
- [00:39] as our awareness of ourselves and our environment.
- [00:43] Its this awareness that allows us to take in and organize information from many sources
- [00:47] and senses, at once.
- [00:49] American psychologist William James thought of consciousness as a continuously moving,
- [00:52] shifting, and unbroken stream, hence the term stream of consciousness.
- [00:56] Others think of it as the brains roving flashlight, shining down an unbroken beam
- [01:00] of light that highlights one thing, and then moves on to the next.
- [01:03] The point is, your conscious experience is forever shifting--for example, right now hopefully
- [01:07] youre focused on the words coming out of my mouth, but with a little shift - your mind
- [01:09] might wander to how you really should shower today, and your chair is uncomfortable, and
- [01:14] you suddenly have to pee, and can you believe what Bernice said?!
- [01:17] Do I smell pizza?
- [01:18] HEY!
- [01:19] EYES HERE!
- [01:20] WERE LEARNING!
- [01:21] Beyond that moment-to-moment shifting, consciousness allows us to contemplate life, think about
- [01:24] infinity, and ride a unicycle across a tightrope while juggling melons, at least in theory.
- [01:30] Our consciousness helps us plan our futures, consider consequences, and reflect on the
- [01:33] past.
- [01:34] It is both the most familiar, and the most mysterious part of our lives.
- [01:38] Its kind of like The Force -- but for the little universes inside our heads.
- [01:46] [INTRO] Throughout our daily lives we flit back and
- [01:53] forth between various states of consciousness, including waking, sleeping, and various altered
- [01:58] states.
- [01:59] These can occur spontaneously, like dreaming, or be physiologically sparked, like a drug-induced
- [02:03] hallucination, or be triggered psychologically, through meditation or hypnosis, for example.
- [02:08] Were going to take the next three episodes to look closely at these different states
- [02:11] of consciousness, but lets start with what it really means to be awake.
- [02:16] For centuries, scientists learned what they could about the brain solely through clinical
- [02:20] observation.
- [02:21] And they learned a lot, for sure, but with todays technology, were actually able
- [02:24] to see some of the structures and activity inside a living, working brain - its electrical,
- [02:30] metabolic, and magnetic signatures displayed on screens for our wonder and amusement.
- [02:34] The field of cognitive neuroscience is the study of how brain activity is linked with
- [02:38] our mental processes, including thinking, perception, memory, and language.
- [02:43] Like other kinds of neuroscience, it uses neuroimaging technologies to consider links
- [02:47] between specific brain states and conscious experiences.
- [02:49] And theres more than one way to scan a brain.
- [02:52] Structural imaging shows the brains anatomy, and is useful in identifying large-scale tumors,
- [02:57] diseases, and injuries.
- [02:59] In contrast, functional imaging shows us electromagnetic or metabolic activity in the brain, like blood
- [03:04] flow, to let us observe correlations between specific mental functions and activity in
- [03:09] particular brain areas.
- [03:11] So, yes, neuroimaging has been revolutionizing the field of psychology, much like telescopes
- [03:15] and microscopes did for astronomy and biology.
- [03:18] But on the other hand, some of this technology is very new, and theres plenty of disagreement
- [03:23] about how to interpret neuroimaging findings.
- [03:25] Remember, correlation does not equal causation.
- [03:28] So, activity in a certain brain region while having certain kinds of thoughts might be
- [03:31] useful to know, but its not the end of the conversation.
- [03:34] Weve already talked a lot about how function is often localized in the brain and how everything
- [03:37] psychological is simultaneously biological--so it stands to reason our thoughts and emotions
- [03:42] could in part be illustrated by a bright flare on a dark screen.
- [03:46] Weve also collected a fair amount of evidence that we dont just have one layer of consciousness
- [03:50] - a single tape playing various tunes - but rather, something more like two layers, each
- [03:54] supported by its own personal bio-psycho-social pit crew.
- [03:59] Im talking about one of the dual process models of consciousness--the idea that our
- [04:03] conscious, deliberate mind could be saying, look!
- [04:05] a squirrel!
- [04:06] while our implicit, automatic mind is simultaneously subprocessing like a computer: color: brown,
- [04:11] tail: bushy, movement: climbing, distance: 20 meters, association: my sister had a squirrel
- [04:15] phobia as a child, implicit bias: I think that squirrels are ruining America.
- [04:20] All of which might weigh upon my behavior upon seeing the little guy.
- [04:23] By some estimates, all your senses are scooping up nearly 11 million bits of information,
- [04:28] EVERY SECOND.
- [04:29] And yet, you consciously register only about 40 at time.
- [04:33] So how do we keep focused and filter out all the chatter to actually get stuff done?
- [04:38] With selective attention, of course!
- [04:40] Selective attention is how we focus our consciousness on one particular stimulus or group of stimuli,
- [04:45] effectively tuning out the rest.
- [04:46] Your consciousness is like a spotlight on a busy stage.
- [04:49] There are other things going on around you that your automatic, subprocessor brain is
- [04:52] covertly registering.
- [04:54] But for those moments when you shine your spotlight, most of the other stimuli fall
- [04:58] away.
- [04:59] Try it at home!
- [05:00] Right now, youre consciously watching this lesson on consciousness.
- [05:03] You probably dont notice the feel of your socks on your feet, or the tongue thats
- [05:08] inside your mouth, always filling up your mouth with tongue!
- [05:11] But as soon as I mention it, the spotlight of your attention turns to them, you feel
- [05:16] those socks on your feet, and youre like wow!
- [05:18] Its weird that theres a tongue in my mouth!
- [05:21] The classic auditory example of selective attention is the cocktail party effect.
- [05:24] You could be in a room with 47 people jabbering away, and yet be able to concentrate your
- [05:29] hearing on one conversation, tuning out the rest of the voices and background music.
- [05:34] But, if the couple next to you were to speak your name, suddenly your cognitive radar would
- [05:39] light up and your attention would whip around to the sound of your name, probably trying
- [05:44] to figure out if Bernice was talking behind your back again.
- [05:47] Bernice!!
- [05:48] This roving spotlight of selective attention is pretty handy most of the time, for spies
- [05:51] and laypeople alike.
- [05:53] But it can also be dangerous, if youre being dumb, and say, texting and driving.
- [05:56] When you shift your primary selective attention from driving to OMG, LOLOLOLOLOL, you also
- [06:02] unwittingly activate your selective inattention, which means that you failed to see that cyclist
- [06:06] who you almost ran over, which would not only have ruined her life but also yours so DONT
- [06:12] TEXT AND DRIVE!
- [06:13] In fact, when your full attention is directed elsewhere, youd be astounded by the scope
- [06:17] of obvious things you fail to notice.
- [06:19] Its called inattentional blindness.
- [06:21] You may have even already been subject to one of the most famous experiments of inattentional
- [06:26] blindness...the Invisible Gorilla or, sometimes, the Moonwalking bear.
- [06:30] Just google either of those things if you want to be tested on your awareness and then
- [06:34] come back.
- [06:37] Pretty great, right?!
- [06:42] Given the prompt to count the number of passes one team makes, your consciousness is focused

- [06:53] also you certainly dont see the dancing gorilla...or bear...whichever one.
- [06:56] The original version of this experiment found that about 50% of people didnt notice that
- [07:01] there was A GORILLA WALKING THROUGH THE ROOM!
- [07:03] THAT is how powerfully selective our attention can be.
- [07:06] Something to remember next time youre behind the wheel.

- [07:13] Except they call it misdirection.

- [07:22] And we cant help but be rubes.
- [07:24] Magicians also prey on our change blindness, the psychological phenomenon in which we fail
- [07:28] to notice changes in our environment.
- [07:29] And no, I dont mean climate change.
- [07:31] I mean the failure to recognize the difference between what was there a moment ago, versus
- [07:35] what is there now.
- [07:36] For example, I have changed shirts several times since this lesson started.
- [07:40] In a well-known and often-copied experiment, sometimes called the person swap, an
- [07:44] experimenter will stop someone in a park and ask for directions.
- [07:47] And then, during some staged interruption, the original experimenter will leave and be
- [07:51] replaced with a totally different person.

- [08:05] notice certain things can be dangerous -- say, faulty memories lead to false eyewitness testimonies
